<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_beryllium" title="Template:Infobox beryllium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_beryllium" title="Template talk:Infobox beryllium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_beryllium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Beryllium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Be</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 4. It is a <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements#Universe" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">relatively rare</a> element in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, usually occurring as a product of the <a href="/wiki/Spallation" title="Spallation">spallation</a> of larger atomic nuclei that have collided with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. Within the cores of stars beryllium is depleted as it is fused and creates larger elements. It is a <a href="/wiki/Divalent" class="mw-redirect" title="Divalent">divalent</a> element which occurs naturally only in combination with other elements in minerals. Notable <a href="/wiki/Gemstones" class="mw-redirect" title="Gemstones">gemstones</a> which contain beryllium include <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryl</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aquamarine_(gemstone)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquamarine (gemstone)">aquamarine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emerald" title="Emerald">emerald</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Chrysoberyl" title="Chrysoberyl">chrysoberyl</a>. As a free <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> it is a steel-gray, strong, lightweight and brittle <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metal</a>.
</p><p>Beryllium improves many physical properties when added as an <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloying</a> element to <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> (notably the alloy <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_copper" title="Beryllium copper">beryllium copper</a>), <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium does not form oxides until it reaches very high temperatures. Tools made of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_copper" title="Beryllium copper">beryllium copper</a> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> are strong and hard and do not create sparks when they strike a steel surface. In structural applications, the combination of high <a href="/wiki/Flexural_rigidity" title="Flexural rigidity">flexural rigidity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_stability" title="Thermal stability">thermal stability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> and low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> (1.85 times that of water) make beryllium metal a desirable <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> material for aircraft components, <a href="/wiki/Missile" title="Missile">missiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Satellites" class="mw-redirect" title="Satellites">satellites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Because of its low density and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a>, beryllium is relatively transparent to X-rays and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>; therefore, it is the most common window material for X-ray equipment and components of <a href="/wiki/Particle_detector" title="Particle detector">particle detectors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The high thermal conductivities of beryllium and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_oxide" title="Beryllium oxide">beryllium oxide</a> have led to their use in thermal management applications.
</p><p>The commercial use of beryllium requires the use of appropriate dust control equipment and industrial controls at all times because of the <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> of inhaled beryllium-containing dusts that can cause a chronic life-threatening allergic disease in some people called <a href="/wiki/Berylliosis" title="Berylliosis">berylliosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>
<p>Beryllium is a steel gray and hard <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> that is brittle at room temperature and has a close-packed hexagonal <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It has exceptional <a href="/wiki/Stiffness" title="Stiffness">stiffness</a> (<a href="/wiki/Young%27s_modulus" title="Young&#39;s modulus">Young's modulus</a> 287 GPa) and a reasonably high <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Modulus_of_elasticity" class="mw-redirect" title="Modulus of elasticity">modulus of elasticity</a> of beryllium is approximately 50% greater than that of steel. The combination of this modulus and a relatively low density results in an unusually fast <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">sound conduction speed</a> in beryllium – about 12.9&#160;km/s at <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">ambient conditions</a>. Other significant properties are high specific heat (1925 J·kg<sup>−1</sup>·K<sup>−1</sup>) and thermal conductivity (216 W·m<sup>−1</sup>·K<sup>−1</sup>), which make beryllium the metal with the best heat dissipation characteristics per unit weight. In combination with the relatively low coefficient of linear <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> (11.4×10<sup>−6</sup> K<sup>−1</sup>), these characteristics result in a unique stability under conditions of thermal loading.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_properties">Nuclear properties</span></h3>
<p>Naturally occurring beryllium, save for slight contamination by the cosmogenic radioisotopes, is isotopically pure beryllium-9, which has a <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">nuclear spin</a> of <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">3</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>. Beryllium has a large scattering cross section for high-energy neutrons, about 6 <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">barns</a> for energies above approximately 10&#160;keV. Therefore, it works as a neutron reflector and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a>, effectively slowing the neutrons to the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_temperature" title="Neutron temperature">thermal energy</a> range of below 0.03&#160;eV, where the total cross section is at least an order of magnitude lower – exact value strongly depends on the purity and size of the crystallites in the material.
</p><p>The single primordial beryllium isotope <sup>9</sup>Be also undergoes a (n,2n) neutron reaction with neutron energies over about 1.9&#160;MeV, to produce <sup>8</sup>Be, which almost immediately breaks into two alpha particles. Thus, for high-energy neutrons, beryllium is a neutron multiplier, releasing more neutrons than it absorbs. This nuclear reaction is:<sup id="cite_ref-BeMelurgy_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeMelurgy-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Be<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + n → 2 <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2 n</dd></dl>
<p>Neutrons are liberated when beryllium <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> are struck by energetic <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a><sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> producing the nuclear reaction
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Be<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + n, where <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is an alpha particle and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is a <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> nucleus.<sup id="cite_ref-BeMelurgy_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeMelurgy-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Beryllium also releases neutrons under bombardment by gamma rays. Thus, natural beryllium bombarded either by alphas or gammas from a suitable radioisotope is a key component of most radioisotope-powered <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a> <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron sources</a> for the laboratory production of free neutrons.
</p><p>Small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> are liberated when <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Be<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> nuclei absorb low energy neutrons in the three-step nuclear reaction
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Be<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + n → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>,&#160;&#160;&#160; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + β<sup>−</sup>,&#160;&#160;&#160; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + n → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Note that <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> has a half-life of only 0.8 seconds, β<sup>−</sup> is an electron, and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> has a high neutron absorption cross-section.  Tritium is a radioisotope of concern in nuclear reactor waste streams.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a metal, beryllium is <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">transparent</a> to most wavelengths of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, making it useful for the output windows of <a href="/wiki/X-ray_tube" title="X-ray tube">X-ray tubes</a> and other such apparatus.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes_and_nucleosynthesis">Isotopes and nucleosynthesis</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_beryllium" title="Isotopes of beryllium">Isotopes of beryllium</a></div>
<p>Both stable and unstable isotopes of beryllium are created in stars, but the radioisotopes do not last long. It is believed that most of the stable beryllium in the universe was originally created in the interstellar medium when <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a> induced fission in heavier elements found in interstellar gas and dust.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Primordial beryllium contains only one stable isotope, <sup>9</sup>Be, and therefore beryllium is a <a href="/wiki/Monoisotopic_element" title="Monoisotopic element">monoisotopic element</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_Activity_Proxies.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Solar_Activity_Proxies.png/300px-Solar_Activity_Proxies.png" width="300" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Solar_Activity_Proxies.png/450px-Solar_Activity_Proxies.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Solar_Activity_Proxies.png 2x" data-file-width="559" data-file-height="361" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_Activity_Proxies.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plot showing variations in solar activity, including variation in sunspot number (red) and <sup>10</sup>Be concentration (blue). Note that the beryllium scale is inverted, so increases on this scale indicate lower <sup>10</sup>Be levels</div></div></div>
<p>Radioactive <a href="/wiki/Cosmogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmogenic">cosmogenic</a> <sup>10</sup>Be is produced in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s atmosphere">atmosphere of the Earth</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200156_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200156-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <sup>10</sup>Be accumulates at the <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> surface, where its relatively long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> (1.36 million years) permits a long <a href="/wiki/Residence_time_(fluid_dynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Residence time (fluid dynamics)">residence time</a> before decaying to <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>-10. Thus, <sup>10</sup>Be and its daughter products are used to examine natural <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Soil formation">soil formation</a> and the development of <a href="/wiki/Laterite" title="Laterite">lateritic soils</a>, and as a <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy</a> for measurement of the variations in <a href="/wiki/Solar_variation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar variation">solar activity</a> and the age of <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The production of <sup>10</sup>Be is inversely proportional to solar activity, because increased <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> during periods of high solar activity decreases the flux of <a href="/wiki/Galactic_cosmic_ray" class="mw-redirect" title="Galactic cosmic ray">galactic cosmic rays</a> that reach the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200156_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200156-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Nuclear explosions also form <sup>10</sup>Be by the reaction of fast neutrons with <sup>13</sup>C in the carbon dioxide in air. This is one of the indicators of past activity at <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapon test">nuclear weapon test</a> sites.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
The isotope <sup>7</sup>Be (half-life 53 days) is also cosmogenic, and shows an atmospheric abundance linked to sunspots, much like <sup>10</sup>Be.
</p><p><sup>8</sup>Be has a very short half-life of about 7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>−<span style="white-space:nowrap">17</span></sup>&#160;s that contributes to its significant cosmological role, as elements heavier than beryllium could not have been produced by nuclear fusion in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> This is due to the lack of sufficient time during the Big Bang's <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> phase to produce carbon by the fusion of <sup>4</sup>He nuclei and the very low concentrations of available <a href="/wiki/Beryllium-8" title="Beryllium-8">beryllium-8</a>. The British <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> Sir <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a> first showed that the energy levels of <sup>8</sup>Be and <sup>12</sup>C allow carbon production by the so-called <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">triple-alpha process</a> in helium-fueled stars where more nucleosynthesis time is available. This process allows carbon to be produced in stars, but not in the Big Bang. Star-created carbon (the basis of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-based_life" title="Carbon-based life">carbon-based life</a>) is thus a component in the elements in the gas and dust ejected by <a href="/wiki/AGB_star" class="mw-redirect" title="AGB star">AGB stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>), as well as the creation of all other elements with <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic numbers</a> larger than that of carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2s electrons of beryllium may contribute to chemical bonding. Therefore, when <sup>7</sup>Be decays by L-<a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>, it does so by taking electrons from its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a> that may be participating in bonding. This makes its decay rate dependent to a measurable degree upon its chemical surroundings&#160;– a rare occurrence in nuclear decay.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The shortest-lived known isotope of beryllium is <sup>13</sup>Be which decays through <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron emission</a>. It has a half-life of 2.7&#160;×&#160;10<sup>−21</sup> s. <sup>6</sup>Be is also very short-lived with a half-life of 5.0&#160;×&#160;10<sup>−21</sup> s.<sup id="cite_ref-crc_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crc-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The exotic isotopes <sup>11</sup>Be and <sup>14</sup>Be are known to exhibit a <a href="/wiki/Halo_nucleus" title="Halo nucleus">nuclear halo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> This phenomenon can be understood as the nuclei of <sup>11</sup>Be and <sup>14</sup>Be have, respectively, 1 and 4 neutrons orbiting substantially outside the classical Fermi 'waterdrop' model of the nucleus.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg/220px-Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg/330px-Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg/440px-Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryllium_OreUSGOV.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beryllium ore with 1US¢ coin for scale</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryl-130023.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Beryl-130023.jpg/120px-Beryl-130023.jpg" width="120" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Beryl-130023.jpg/180px-Beryl-130023.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Beryl-130023.jpg/240px-Beryl-130023.jpg 2x" data-file-width="253" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryl-130023.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emerald" title="Emerald">Emerald</a> is a naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compound</a> of beryllium.</div></div></div>
<p>The Sun has a concentration of 0.1 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per billion">parts per billion</a> (ppb) of beryllium.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium has a concentration of 2 to 6 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (ppm) in the Earth's crust.<sup id="cite_ref-Merck_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  It is most concentrated in the soils, 6 ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200159_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200159-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Trace amounts of <sup>9</sup>Be are found in the Earth's atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200159_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200159-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The concentration of beryllium in sea water is 0.2–0.6 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_trillion" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per trillion">parts per trillion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200159_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200159-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In stream water, however, beryllium is more abundant with a concentration of 0.1 ppb.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beryllium is found in over 100 minerals,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> but most are uncommon to rare. The more common beryllium containing minerals include: <a href="/wiki/Bertrandite" title="Bertrandite">bertrandite</a> (Be<sub>4</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryl</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>Be<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>6</sub>O<sub>18</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Chrysoberyl" title="Chrysoberyl">chrysoberyl</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>BeO<sub>4</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Phenakite" title="Phenakite">phenakite</a> (Be<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>). Precious forms of beryl are <a href="/wiki/Aquamarine_(gemstone)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquamarine (gemstone)">aquamarine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_beryl" class="mw-redirect" title="Red beryl">red beryl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emerald" title="Emerald">emerald</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> 
The green color in gem-quality forms of beryl comes from varying amounts of chromium (about 2% for emerald).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two main ores of beryllium, beryl and bertrandite, are found in Argentina, Brazil, India, Madagascar, Russia and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Total world reserves of beryllium ore are greater than 400,000 tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<p>The extraction of beryllium from its compounds is a difficult process due to its high affinity for oxygen at elevated temperatures, and its ability to reduce water when its oxide film is removed. The United States, China and Kazakhstan are the only three countries involved in the industrial-scale extraction of beryllium.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium production technology is in early stages of development in Russia after a 20-year hiatus.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beryllium is most commonly extracted from the mineral <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryl</a>, which is either <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintered</a> using an extraction agent or melted into a soluble mixture. The sintering process involves mixing beryl with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluorosilicate" title="Sodium fluorosilicate">sodium fluorosilicate</a> and soda at 770&#160;°C (1,420&#160;°F) to form <a href="/wiki/Tetrafluoroberyllate" title="Tetrafluoroberyllate">sodium fluoroberyllate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_hydroxide" title="Beryllium hydroxide">Beryllium hydroxide</a> is precipitated from a solution of sodium fluoroberyllate and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> in water. Extraction of beryllium using the melt method involves grinding beryl into a powder and heating it to 1,650&#160;°C (3,000&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The melt is quickly cooled with water and then reheated 250 to 300&#160;°C (482 to 572&#160;°F) in concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, mostly yielding <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_sulfate" title="Beryllium sulfate">beryllium sulfate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">aluminium sulfate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Aqueous <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> is then used to remove the aluminium and sulfur, leaving beryllium hydroxide.
</p><p>Beryllium hydroxide created using either the sinter or melt method is then converted into <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_fluoride" title="Beryllium fluoride">beryllium fluoride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_chloride" title="Beryllium chloride">beryllium chloride</a>. To form the fluoride, aqueous ammonium hydrogen fluoride is added to beryllium hydroxide to yield a precipitate of ammonium tetrafluoroberyllate, which is heated to 1,000&#160;°C (1,830&#160;°F) to form beryllium fluoride.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Heating the fluoride to 900&#160;°C (1,650&#160;°F) with <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> forms finely divided beryllium, and additional heating to 1,300&#160;°C (2,370&#160;°F) creates the compact metal.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Heating beryllium hydroxide forms the oxide, which becomes beryllium chloride when combined with carbon and chlorine. <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">Electrolysis</a> of molten beryllium chloride is then used to obtain the metal.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Beryllium_compounds" title="Category:Beryllium compounds">Category:Beryllium compounds</a>.</div>
<p>Beryllium's chemical behavior is largely a result of its small <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">atomic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">ionic</a> radii. It thus has very high <a href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionization potential">ionization potentials</a> and strong polarization while bonded to other atoms, which is why all of its compounds are <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It is more chemically similar to aluminium than its close neighbors in the periodic table due to having a similar charge-to-radius ratio.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
An oxide layer forms around beryllium that prevents further reactions with air unless heated above 1000&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Once ignited, beryllium burns brilliantly forming a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_oxide" title="Beryllium oxide">beryllium oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_nitride" title="Beryllium nitride">beryllium nitride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium dissolves readily in non-<a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_acid" title="Oxidizing acid">oxidizing acids</a>, such as HCl and diluted H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, but not in <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> or water as this forms the oxide.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This behavior is similar to that of aluminium metal. Beryllium also dissolves in alkali solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BeHydrolysis.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/BeHydrolysis.png/220px-BeHydrolysis.png" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1f/BeHydrolysis.png 1.5x" data-file-width="282" data-file-height="280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BeHydrolysis.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beryllium hydrolysis as a function of pH<br />Water molecules attached to Be are omitted</div></div></div>
<p>The beryllium atom has the electronic configuration [He] 2s<sup>2</sup>. The two valence electrons give beryllium a +2 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> and thus the ability to form two <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>; the only evidence of lower valence of beryllium is in the solubility of the metal in <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_dichloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Beryllium dichloride">BeCl<sub>2</sub></a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wiberg&amp;Holleman_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiberg&amp;Holleman-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and in two neutral beryllium bis(carbene) compounds in which the Be center bears a formal oxidation state of zero.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/Octet_rule" title="Octet rule">octet rule</a>, atoms tend to seek a valence of 8 in order to resemble a <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a>. Beryllium tries to achieve a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of 4 because its two covalent bonds fill half of this octet.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Tetracoordination allows beryllium compounds, such as the fluoride or chloride, to form polymers.
</p><p>This characteristic is employed in analytical techniques using <a href="/wiki/EDTA" class="mw-redirect" title="EDTA">EDTA</a> as a ligand. EDTA preferentially forms <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral complexes</a>&#160;– thus absorbing other cations such as Al<sup>3+</sup> which might interfere – for example, in the <a href="/wiki/Liquid-liquid_extraction" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid-liquid extraction">solvent extraction</a> of a complex formed between Be<sup>2+</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylacetone" title="Acetylacetone">acetylacetone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium(II) readily forms complexes with strong donating ligands such as phosphine oxides and arsine oxides. There have been extensive studies of these complexes which show the stability of the O-Be bond.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Solutions of beryllium salts, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_sulfate" title="Beryllium sulfate">beryllium sulfate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_nitrate" title="Beryllium nitrate">beryllium nitrate</a>, are acidic because of hydrolysis of the [Be(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> ion.
</p>
<dl><dd>[Be(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ [Be(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>(OH)]<sup>+</sup> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Other products of hydrolysis include the <a href="/wiki/Trimer_(chemistry)" title="Trimer (chemistry)">trimeric</a> ion [Be<sub>3</sub>(OH)<sub>3</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup>. <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_hydroxide" title="Beryllium hydroxide">Beryllium hydroxide</a>, Be(OH)<sub>2</sub>, is insoluble even in acidic solutions with pH less than 6, that is at biological pH. It is <a href="/wiki/Amphoterism" title="Amphoterism">amphoteric</a> and dissolves in strongly <a href="/wiki/Alkalinity" title="Alkalinity">alkaline</a> solutions.
</p><p>Beryllium forms <a href="/wiki/Binary_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary compound">binary compounds</a> with many non-metals. <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">Anhydrous</a> <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a> are known for <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">F</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">Cl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">Br</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">I</a>. <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_fluoride" title="Beryllium fluoride">BeF<sub>2</sub></a> has a <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a>-like structure with corner-shared BeF<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_chloride" title="Beryllium chloride">BeCl<sub>2</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_bromide" title="Beryllium bromide">BeBr<sub>2</sub></a> have chain structures with edge-shared tetrahedra. All beryllium halides have a linear monomeric molecular structure in the gas phase.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beryllium_difluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Beryllium difluoride">Beryllium difluoride</a>, BeF<sub>2</sub>, is different than the other <a href="/wiki/Difluoride" title="Difluoride">difluorides</a>.  In general, beryllium has a tendency to bond covalently, much more so than the other <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earths</a> and its fluoride is partially covalent (although still more ionic than its other halides).  BeF<sub>2</sub> has many similarities to <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">SiO<sub>2</sub></a> (<a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>) a mostly covalently bonded <a href="/wiki/Network_solid" class="mw-redirect" title="Network solid">network solid</a>.  BeF<sub>2</sub> has tetrahedrally coordinated metal and forms glasses (is difficult to crystallize).  When crystalline, beryllium fluoride has the same room temperature crystal structure as quartz and shares many higher temperature structures also.  Beryllium difluoride is very soluble in water,<sup id="cite_ref-dissolve_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dissolve-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> unlike the other alkaline earth difluorides. (Although they are strongly ionic, they do not dissolve because of the especially strong <a href="/wiki/Madelung_constant" title="Madelung constant">lattice energy</a> of the fluorite structure.)  However, BeF<sub>2</sub> has much lower electrical conductivity when in solution or when molten than would be expected if it were fully ionic.<sup id="cite_ref-Beryllium_halide_and_pseudohalides_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beryllium_halide_and_pseudohalides-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beryllium_chemistry_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beryllium_chemistry-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMackayMackayHenderson2002243–244_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackayMackayHenderson2002243–244-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-General_analytical_chemistry_of_beryllium_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-General_analytical_chemistry_of_beryllium-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table style="margin:1em auto 1em auto; text-align:left; width:540px;" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<td style="width:220px; padding-left:50px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BeF2_glass.svg" class="image"><img alt="cube of 8 yellow atoms with white ones at the holes of the yellow structure" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/BeF2_glass.svg/475px-BeF2_glass.svg.png" width="475" height="500" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/BeF2_glass.svg/713px-BeF2_glass.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/BeF2_glass.svg/950px-BeF2_glass.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="475" data-file-height="500" /></a>

<tr>

</td>
