</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Beryllium_oxide" title="Beryllium oxide">Beryllium oxide</a>, BeO, is a white <a href="/wiki/Refractory" title="Refractory">refractory</a> solid, which has the <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">wurtzite</a> crystal structure and a thermal conductivity as high as in some metals. BeO is <a href="/wiki/Amphoteric" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a>. Salts of beryllium can be produced by treating Be(OH)<sub>2</sub> with acid.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">telluride</a> are known, all having the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system#Zincblende_structure" title="Cubic crystal system">zincblende structure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiberg&amp;Holleman_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiberg&amp;Holleman-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beryllium_nitride" title="Beryllium nitride">Beryllium nitride</a>, Be<sub>3</sub>N<sub>2</sub> is a high-melting-point compound which is readily hydrolyzed. Beryllium azide, BeN<sub>6</sub> is known and beryllium phosphide, Be<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub> has a similar structure to Be<sub>3</sub>N<sub>2</sub>. Basic beryllium nitrate and <a href="/wiki/Basic_beryllium_acetate" title="Basic beryllium acetate">basic beryllium acetate</a> have similar tetrahedral structures with four beryllium atoms coordinated to a central oxide ion.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiberg&amp;Holleman_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiberg&amp;Holleman-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> A number of beryllium <a href="/wiki/Boride" title="Boride">borides</a> are known, such as Be<sub>5</sub>B, Be<sub>4</sub>B, Be<sub>2</sub>B, BeB<sub>2</sub>, BeB<sub>6</sub> and BeB<sub>12</sub>. <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_carbide" title="Beryllium carbide">Beryllium carbide</a>, Be<sub>2</sub>C, is a refractory brick-red compound that reacts with water to give <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiberg&amp;Holleman_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiberg&amp;Holleman-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> No beryllium <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicide</a> has been identified.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>The mineral <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryl</a>, which contains beryllium, has been used at least since the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Ptolemaic dynasty</a> of Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968535_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968535-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In the first century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, Roman naturalist <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> mentioned in his encyclopedia <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i> that beryl and <a href="/wiki/Emerald" title="Emerald">emerald</a> ("smaragdus") were similar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968536_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968536-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Graecus_Holmiensis" title="Papyrus Graecus Holmiensis">Papyrus Graecus Holmiensis</a>, written in the third or fourth century CE, contains notes on how to prepare artificial emerald and beryl.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968536_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968536-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg/170px-Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg" width="170" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg/255px-Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg/340px-Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="758" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louis_Nicolas_Vauquelin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis-Nicolas_Vauquelin" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis-Nicolas Vauquelin">Louis-Nicolas Vauquelin</a> discovered beryllium</div></div></div>
<p>Early analyses of emeralds and beryls by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Torbern_Olof_Bergman" class="mw-redirect" title="Torbern Olof Bergman">Torbern Olof Bergman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Karl_Achard" title="Franz Karl Achard">Franz Karl Achard</a>, and Johann Jakob Bindheim always yielded similar elements, leading to the fallacious conclusion that both substances are <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_silicate" title="Aluminium silicate">aluminium silicates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968537_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968537-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Mineralogist <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Just_Ha%C3%BCy" title="René Just Haüy">René Just Haüy</a> discovered that both crystals are geometrically identical, and he asked chemist <a href="/wiki/Louis-Nicolas_Vauquelin" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis-Nicolas Vauquelin">Louis-Nicolas Vauquelin</a> for a chemical analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968535_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968535-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 1798 paper read before the <a href="/wiki/Institut_de_France" title="Institut de France">Institut de France</a>, Vauquelin reported that he found a new "earth" by dissolving <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">aluminium hydroxide</a> from emerald and beryl in an additional <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The editors of the journal <i>Annales de Chimie et de Physique</i> named the new earth "glucine" for the sweet taste of some of its compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Klaproth preferred the name "beryllina" due to the fact that <a href="/wiki/Yttria" class="mw-redirect" title="Yttria">yttria</a> also formed sweet salts.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968538_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968538-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  The name "beryllium" was first used by Wöhler in 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg/170px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg/255px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg/340px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg 2x" data-file-width="891" data-file-height="1158" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Stich.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> was one of the men who independently isolated beryllium</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Bussy" title="Antoine Bussy">Antoine Bussy</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> independently isolated beryllium in 1828 by the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reaction</a> of metallic <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> with <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_chloride" title="Beryllium chloride">beryllium chloride</a>, as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd>BeCl<sub>2</sub> + 2 K → 2 KCl + Be</dd></dl>
<p>Using an alcohol lamp, Wöhler heated alternating layers of beryllium chloride and potassium in a wired-shut platinum crucible. The above reaction immediately took place and caused the crucible to become white hot. Upon cooling and washing the resulting gray-black powder he saw that it was made of fine particles with a dark metallic luster.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968539_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968539-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The highly reactive potassium had been produced by the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of its compounds, a process discovered 21 years before. The chemical method using potassium yielded only small grains of beryllium from which no ingot of metal could be cast or hammered.
</p><p>The direct electrolysis of a molten mixture of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_fluoride" title="Beryllium fluoride">beryllium fluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">sodium fluoride</a> by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lebeau" title="Paul Lebeau">Paul Lebeau</a> in 1898 resulted in the first pure (99.5 to 99.8%) samples of beryllium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968539_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968539-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> However, industrial production started only after the First World War. The original industrial involvement included subsidiaries and scientists related to the Union Carbide and Carbon Corporation in Cleveland OH and Siemens &amp; Halske AG in Berlin. In the US, the process was ruled by Hugh S. Cooper, director of The Kemet Laboratories Company. In Germany, the first commercially successful process for producing beryllium was developed in 1921 by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Stock" title="Alfred Stock">Alfred Stock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Goldschmidt" title="Hans Goldschmidt">Hans Goldschmidt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A sample of beryllium was bombarded with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_ray" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha ray">alpha rays</a> from the decay of <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> in a 1932 experiment by <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> that uncovered the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> This same method is used in one class of radioisotope-based laboratory <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron sources</a> that produce 30 neutrons for every million α particles.<sup id="cite_ref-Merck_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beryllium production saw a rapid increase during World War II, due to the rising demand for hard beryllium-copper alloys and <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a> for <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent light">fluorescent lights</a>. Most early fluorescent lamps used <a href="/wiki/Zinc_orthosilicate" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc orthosilicate">zinc orthosilicate</a> with varying content of beryllium to emit greenish light. Small additions of magnesium <a href="/wiki/Tungstate" title="Tungstate">tungstate</a> improved the blue part of the spectrum to yield an acceptable white light. Halophosphate-based phosphors replaced beryllium-based phosphors after beryllium was found to be toxic.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Electrolysis of a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_fluoride" title="Beryllium fluoride">beryllium fluoride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">sodium fluoride</a> was used to isolate beryllium during the 19th century. The metal's high melting point makes this process more energy-consuming than corresponding processes used for the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a>. Early in the 20th century, the production of beryllium by the thermal decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_iodide" title="Beryllium iodide">beryllium iodide</a> was investigated following the success of a similar process for the production of <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, but this process proved to be uneconomical for volume production.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure beryllium metal did not become readily available until 1957, even though it had been used as an alloying metal to harden and toughen copper much earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium could be produced by reducing beryllium compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_chloride" title="Beryllium chloride">beryllium chloride</a> with metallic potassium or sodium. Currently most beryllium is produced by reducing beryllium fluoride with purified <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>. The price on the American market for <a href="/wiki/Casting_(metalworking)" title="Casting (metalworking)">vacuum-cast</a> beryllium ingots was about $338 per pound ($745 per kilogram) in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1998 and 2008, the world's production of beryllium had decreased from 343 to about 200 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, of which 176 tonnes (88%) came from the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSMCS2000_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSMCS2000-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USGSMCS2010_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSMCS2010-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>Early precursors of the word <i>beryllium</i> can be traced to many languages, including <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">beryllus</i>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i lang="fr" title="French language text">béry</i>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">βήρυλλος</span>, <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">bērullos</i>, 'beryl'; <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> <span lang="pra" title="Prakrit languages collective text">वॆरुलिय‌</span> (<i lang="pra-Latn" title="Prakrit languages collective text">veruliya</i>); <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" class="mw-redirect" title="Pāli">Pāli</a> <span lang="pi" title="Pali language text">वेलुरिय</span> (<i lang="pi-Latn" title="Pali language text">veḷuriya</i>), <span lang="pi" title="Pali language text">भेलिरु</span> (<i lang="pi-Latn" title="Pali language text">veḷiru</i>) or <span lang="pi" title="Pali language text">भिलर्</span> (<i lang="pi-Latn" title="Pali language text">viḷar</i>) – "to become pale", in reference to the pale semiprecious gemstone beryl. The original source is probably the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <span lang="sa" title="Sanskrit language text">वैडूर्य</span> (<i lang="sa-Latn" title="Sanskrit language text">vaidurya</i>), which is of <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">South Indian</a> origin and could be related to the name of the modern city of <a href="/wiki/Belur,_Karnataka" title="Belur, Karnataka">Belur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> For about 160 years, beryllium was also known as <span id="Glucinium"></span> <b>glucinum</b> or <b>glucinium</b> (with the accompanying chemical symbol "<b>Gl</b>",<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> or "<b>G</b>" <sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>), the name coming from the Ancient Greek word for sweet: <span lang="grc"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%CE%BB%CF%85%CE%BA%CF%8D%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" class="extiw" title="wikt:γλυκύς">γλυκύς</a></span>, due to the sweet taste of beryllium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_windows">Radiation windows</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryllium_target.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Beryllium_target.jpg/220px-Beryllium_target.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Beryllium_target.jpg/330px-Beryllium_target.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Beryllium_target.jpg/440px-Beryllium_target.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryllium_target.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beryllium target which "converts" a proton beam into a neutron beam</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Be_foil_square.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Be_foil_square.jpg/220px-Be_foil_square.jpg" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Be_foil_square.jpg/330px-Be_foil_square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Be_foil_square.jpg/440px-Be_foil_square.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1192" data-file-height="1003" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Be_foil_square.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A square beryllium foil mounted in a steel case to be used as a window between a vacuum chamber and an <a href="/wiki/X-ray_microscope" title="X-ray microscope">X-ray microscope</a>. Beryllium is highly transparent to X-rays owing to its low <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Because of its low atomic number and very low absorption for X-rays, the oldest and still one of the most important applications of beryllium is in radiation windows for <a href="/wiki/X-ray_tube" title="X-ray tube">X-ray tubes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Extreme demands are placed on purity and cleanliness of beryllium to avoid artifacts in the X-ray images. Thin beryllium foils are used as radiation windows for X-ray detectors, and the extremely low absorption minimizes the heating effects caused by high intensity, low energy X-rays typical of <a href="/wiki/Synchrotron" title="Synchrotron">synchrotron</a> radiation. Vacuum-tight windows and beam-tubes for radiation experiments on synchrotrons are manufactured exclusively from beryllium. In scientific setups for various X-ray emission studies (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Energy-dispersive_X-ray_spectroscopy" title="Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy">energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy</a>) the sample holder is usually made of beryllium because its emitted X-rays have much lower energies (~100&#160;eV) than X-rays from most studied materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Low <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> also makes beryllium relatively transparent to energetic <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">particles</a>. Therefore, it is used to build the <a href="/wiki/Beamline" title="Beamline">beam pipe</a> around the collision region in <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> setups, such as all four main detector experiments at the <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> (<a href="/wiki/A_Large_Ion_Collider_Experiment" class="mw-redirect" title="A Large Ion Collider Experiment">ALICE</a>, <a href="/wiki/ATLAS_experiment" title="ATLAS experiment">ATLAS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compact_Muon_Solenoid" title="Compact Muon Solenoid">CMS</a>, <a href="/wiki/LHCb" class="mw-redirect" title="LHCb">LHCb</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Tevatron" title="Tevatron">Tevatron</a> and the <a href="/wiki/SLAC" class="mw-redirect" title="SLAC">SLAC</a>. The low density of beryllium allows collision products to reach the surrounding detectors without significant interaction, its stiffness allows a powerful vacuum to be produced within the pipe to minimize interaction with gases, its thermal stability allows it to function correctly at temperatures of only a few degrees above <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>, and its <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a> nature keeps it from interfering with the complex multipole magnet systems used to steer and <a href="/wiki/Strong_focusing" title="Strong focusing">focus</a> the <a href="/wiki/Particle_beam" title="Particle beam">particle beams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_applications">Mechanical applications</span></h3>
<p>Because of its stiffness, light weight and dimensional stability over a wide temperature range, beryllium metal is used for lightweight structural components in the defense and <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> industries in high-speed <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guided_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Guided missile">guided missiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a>. Several <a href="/wiki/Liquid-fuel_rocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid-fuel rocket">liquid-fuel rockets</a> have used <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_nozzle" title="Rocket engine nozzle">rocket nozzles</a> made of pure beryllium.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium powder was itself studied as a <a href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Rocket fuel">rocket fuel</a>, but this use has never materialized.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> A small number of extreme high-end <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_frame" title="Bicycle frame">bicycle frames</a> have been built with beryllium.<sup id="cite_ref-museum_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-museum-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> From 1998 to 2000, the <a href="/wiki/McLaren" title="McLaren">McLaren</a> <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> team used <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> engines with beryllium-aluminium-<a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> pistons.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The use of beryllium engine components was banned following a protest by <a href="/wiki/Scuderia_Ferrari" title="Scuderia Ferrari">Scuderia Ferrari</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mixing about 2.0% beryllium into <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> forms an <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> called <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_copper" title="Beryllium copper">beryllium copper</a> that is six times stronger than copper alone.<sup id="cite_ref-McGraw-Hill2004_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw-Hill2004-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium alloys are used in many applications because of their combination of elasticity, high <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, high strength and <a href="/wiki/Hardness_(materials_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardness (materials science)">hardness</a>, nonmagnetic properties, as well as good <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(material)" title="Fatigue (material)">fatigue resistance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> These applications include non-sparking tools that are used near flammable gases (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Beryllium_nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Beryllium nickel (page does not exist)">beryllium nickel</a>), in <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">springs</a> and membranes (beryllium nickel and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Beryllium_iron&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Beryllium iron (page does not exist)">beryllium iron</a>) used in surgical instruments and high temperature devices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158_27-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200158-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> As little as 50 parts per million of beryllium alloyed with liquid <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> leads to a significant increase in oxidation resistance and decrease in flammability.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg/220px-Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg" width="220" height="61" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg/330px-Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg/440px-Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg 2x" data-file-width="994" data-file-height="276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beryllium_Copper_Adjustable_Wrench.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beryllium Copper Adjustable Wrench</div></div></div>
<p>The high elastic stiffness of beryllium has led to its extensive use in precision instrumentation, e.g. in  <a href="/wiki/Inertial_guidance" class="mw-redirect" title="Inertial guidance">inertial guidance</a> systems and in the support mechanisms for optical systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium-copper alloys were also applied as a hardening agent in "Jason pistols", which were used to strip the paint from the hulls of ships.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beryllium was also used for cantilevers in high performance phonograph cartridge styli, where its extreme stiffness and low density allowed for tracking weights to be reduced to 1 gram, yet still track high frequency passages with minimal distortion.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An earlier major application of beryllium was in <a href="/wiki/Brake" title="Brake">brakes</a> for military <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplanes</a> because of its hardness, high melting point, and exceptional ability to <a href="/wiki/Heat_dissipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat dissipation">dissipate heat</a>. Environmental considerations have led to substitution by other materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To reduce costs, beryllium can be <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloyed</a> with significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/AlBeMet" title="AlBeMet">AlBeMet</a> alloy  (a trade name). This blend is cheaper than pure beryllium, while still retaining many desirable properties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mirrors">Mirrors</span></h3>
<p>Beryllium <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirrors</a> are of particular interest. Large-area mirrors, frequently with a <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb_mirror" title="Honeycomb mirror">honeycomb support structure</a>, are used, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Meteorological_satellite" class="mw-redirect" title="Meteorological satellite">meteorological satellites</a> where low weight and long-term dimensional stability are critical. Smaller beryllium mirrors are used in <a href="/wiki/Control_system" title="Control system">optical guidance</a> systems and in <a href="/wiki/Fire-control_system" title="Fire-control system">fire-control systems</a>, e.g. in the German-made <a href="/wiki/Leopard_1" title="Leopard 1">Leopard 1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leopard_2" title="Leopard 2">Leopard 2</a> <a href="/wiki/Main_battle_tank" title="Main battle tank">main battle tanks</a>. In these systems, very rapid movement of the mirror is required which again dictates low mass and high rigidity. Usually the beryllium mirror is coated with hard <a href="/wiki/Electroless_nickel_plating" title="Electroless nickel plating">electroless nickel plating</a> which can be more easily polished to a finer optical finish than beryllium. In some applications, though, the beryllium blank is polished without any coating. This is particularly applicable to <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> operation where thermal expansion mismatch can cause the coating to buckle.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> will have 18 hexagonal beryllium sections for its mirrors. Because JWST will face a temperature of 33 K, the mirror is made of gold-plated beryllium, capable of handling extreme cold better than glass. Beryllium contracts and deforms less than glass – and remains more uniform – in such temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> For the same reason, the optics of the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> are entirely built of beryllium metal.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_applications">Magnetic applications</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG/220px-BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG/330px-BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG/440px-BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BERYLLIUM_-_KUGEL_1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sphere Beryllium B52 - <a href="/wiki/Gyrocompass" title="Gyrocompass">Gyrocompass</a></div></div></div>
<p>Beryllium is non-magnetic. Therefore, tools fabricated out of beryllium-based materials are used by naval or military <a href="/wiki/Explosive_ordnance_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Explosive ordnance disposal">explosive ordnance disposal</a> teams for work on or near <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">naval mines</a>, since these mines commonly have <a href="/wiki/Fuze" title="Fuze">magnetic fuzes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> They are also found in maintenance and construction materials near <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a> (MRI) machines because of the high magnetic fields generated.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In the fields of <a href="/wiki/Radio_communication" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio communication">radio communications</a> and powerful (usually military) <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radars</a>, hand tools made of beryllium are used to tune the highly magnetic <a href="/wiki/Klystron" title="Klystron">klystrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnetron" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetron">magnetrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traveling_wave_tube" class="mw-redirect" title="Traveling wave tube">traveling wave tubes</a>, etc., that are used for generating high levels of <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> power in the <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_applications">Nuclear applications</span></h3>
<p>Thin plates or foils of beryllium are sometimes used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_design" title="Nuclear weapon design">nuclear weapon designs</a> as the very outer layer of the <a href="/wiki/Plutonium_pit" class="mw-redirect" title="Plutonium pit">plutonium pits</a> in the primary stages of <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermonuclear bomb">thermonuclear bombs</a>, placed to surround the <a href="/wiki/Fissile" class="mw-redirect" title="Fissile">fissile</a> material. These layers of beryllium are good "pushers" for the <a href="/wiki/Implosion_(mechanical_process)" title="Implosion (mechanical process)">implosion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>, and they are good <a href="/wiki/Neutron_reflector" title="Neutron reflector">neutron reflectors</a>, just as in beryllium-moderated <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactors" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear reactors">nuclear reactors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-weapons_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weapons-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beryllium is also commonly used in some <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron sources</a> in laboratory devices in which relatively few neutrons are needed (rather than having to use a nuclear reactor, or a <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerator</a>-powered <a href="/wiki/Neutron_generator" title="Neutron generator">neutron generator</a>). For this purpose, a target of beryllium-9 is bombarded with energetic alpha particles from a <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a>-210, <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>-226, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>-238, or <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a>-241. In the nuclear reaction that occurs, a beryllium nucleus is <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmuted</a> into carbon-12, and one free neutron is emitted, traveling in about the same direction as the alpha particle was heading. Such <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> driven beryllium neutron sources, named <a href="/wiki/Modulated_neutron_initiator" title="Modulated neutron initiator">"urchin"</a> neutron initiators, were used in some early <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bombs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-weapons_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weapons-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Neutron sources in which beryllium is bombarded with <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Gamma_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma decay">gamma decay</a> radioisotope, are also used to produce laboratory neutrons.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:251px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CANDU_fuel_bundles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/CANDU_fuel_bundles.jpg" width="249" height="139" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="139" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CANDU_fuel_bundles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two CANDU fuel bundles: Each about 50&#160;cm in length and 10&#160;cm in diameter.  Notice the small appendages on the fuel clad surfaces</div></div></div>
<p>Beryllium is also used in fuel fabrication for <a href="/wiki/CANDU" class="mw-redirect" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactors.  The fuel elements have small appendages that are resistance brazed to the fuel cladding using an induction brazing process with Be as the braze filler material.  Bearing pads are brazed in place to prevent fuel bundle to pressure tube contact, and inter-element spacer pads are brazed on to prevent element to element contact.
</p><p>Beryllium is also used at the <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">Joint European Torus</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear-fusion research laboratory</a>, and it will be used in the more advanced <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> to condition the components which face the plasma.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium has also been proposed as a <a href="/wiki/Cladding_(nuclear_fuel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cladding (nuclear fuel)">cladding</a> material for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_rod" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear fuel rod">nuclear fuel rods</a>, because of its good combination of mechanical, chemical, and nuclear properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Beryllium_fluoride" title="Beryllium fluoride">Beryllium fluoride</a> is one of the constituent salts of the eutectic salt mixture <a href="/wiki/FLiBe" title="FLiBe">FLiBe</a>, which is used as a solvent, moderator and coolant in many hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">molten salt reactor</a> designs, including the <a href="/wiki/Liquid_fluoride_thorium_reactor" title="Liquid fluoride thorium reactor">liquid fluoride thorium reactor</a> (LFTR).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustics">Acoustics</span></h3>
<p>The low weight and high rigidity of beryllium make it useful as a material for high-frequency <a href="/wiki/Speaker_driver" title="Speaker driver">speaker drivers</a>. Because beryllium is expensive (many times more than <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>), hard to shape due to its brittleness, and toxic if mishandled, beryllium <a href="/wiki/Tweeter" title="Tweeter">tweeters</a> are limited to high-end home,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pro_audio" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro audio">pro audio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Public_address" class="mw-redirect" title="Public address">public address</a> applications.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Some high-fidelity products have been fraudulently claimed to be made of the material.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some high-end <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_cartridge" title="Magnetic cartridge">phonograph cartridges</a> used beryllium cantilevers to improve tracking by reducing mass.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic">Electronic</span></h3>
<p>Beryllium is a <a href="/wiki/P-type_semiconductor" class="mw-redirect" title="P-type semiconductor">p-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopant</a> in <a href="/wiki/List_of_semiconductor_materials" title="List of semiconductor materials">III-V compound semiconductors</a>. It is widely used in materials such as <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">GaAs</a>, <a href="/wiki/AlGaAs" class="mw-redirect" title="AlGaAs">AlGaAs</a>, <a href="/wiki/InGaAs" class="mw-redirect" title="InGaAs">InGaAs</a> and <a href="/wiki/InAlAs" class="mw-redirect" title="InAlAs">InAlAs</a> grown by <a href="/wiki/Molecular_beam_epitaxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular beam epitaxy">molecular beam epitaxy</a> (MBE).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Cross-rolled beryllium sheet is an excellent structural support for <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">printed circuit boards</a> in <a href="/wiki/Surface-mount_technology" title="Surface-mount technology">surface-mount technology</a>. In critical electronic applications, beryllium is both a structural support and <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>. The application also requires a coefficient of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> that is well matched to the alumina and <a href="/wiki/Glass-reinforced_plastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass-reinforced plastic">polyimide-glass</a> <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(materials_science)" title="Substrate (materials science)">substrates</a>. The beryllium-beryllium oxide <a href="/wiki/Metal_matrix_composite" title="Metal matrix composite">composite</a> "<a href="/wiki/E-Material" title="E-Material">E-Materials</a>" have been specially designed for these electronic applications and have the additional advantage that the thermal expansion coefficient can be tailored to match diverse substrate materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Be_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Be-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beryllium_oxide" title="Beryllium oxide">Beryllium oxide</a> is useful for many applications that require the combined properties of an <a href="/wiki/Electrical_insulator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical insulator">electrical insulator</a> and an excellent heat conductor, with high strength and hardness, and a very high melting point. Beryllium oxide is frequently used as an insulator base plate in <a href="/wiki/Power_semiconductor_device" title="Power semiconductor device">high-power</a> <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">radio frequency</a> <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitters</a> for telecommunications. Beryllium oxide is also being studied for use in increasing the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> pellets.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium compounds were used in <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent light">fluorescent lighting</a> tubes, but this use was discontinued because of the disease <a href="/wiki/Berylliosis" title="Berylliosis">berylliosis</a> which developed in the workers who were making the tubes.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">Healthcare</span></h3>
<p>Beryllium is a component of several <a href="/wiki/Dental_material" title="Dental material">dental alloys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ElshahawyWatanabe2014_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ElshahawyWatanabe2014-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupational_safety_and_health">Occupational safety and health</span></h2>
<p>Beryllium is a health and safety issue for workers. Exposure to beryllium in the workplace can lead to a sensitization immune response and can over time develop <a href="/wiki/Berylliosis" title="Berylliosis">chronic beryllium disease</a> (CBD).<sup id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) in the United States researches these effects in collaboration with a major manufacturer of beryllium products. The goal of this research is to prevent sensitization and CBD by developing a better understanding of the work processes and exposures that may present a potential risk for workers, and to develop effective interventions that will reduce the risk for adverse health effects. NIOSH also conducts genetic research on sensitization and CBD, independently of this collaboration.<sup id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdc.gov-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The NIOSH Manual of Analytical Methods contains methods for measuring occupational exposures to beryllium.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span></h2>

</td></tr>




</tbody></table>
<p>Approximately 35&#160;micrograms of beryllium is found in the average human body, an amount not considered harmful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200157_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200157-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium is chemically similar to <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and therefore can displace it from <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>, which causes them to malfunction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200157_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200157-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Because Be<sup>2+</sup> is a highly charged and small ion, it can easily get into many tissues and cells, where it specifically targets cell nuclei, inhibiting many enzymes, including those used for synthesizing DNA. Its toxicity is exacerbated by the fact that the body has no means to control beryllium levels, and once inside the body the beryllium cannot be removed.<sup id="cite_ref-Venugopal_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Venugopal-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Chronic <a href="/wiki/Berylliosis" title="Berylliosis">berylliosis</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulmonary">pulmonary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Systemic_circulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Systemic circulation">systemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Granuloma" title="Granuloma">granulomatous</a> disease caused by inhalation of dust or fumes contaminated with beryllium; either large amounts over a short time or small amounts over a long time can lead to this ailment. Symptoms of the disease can take up to five years to develop; about a third of patients with it die and the survivors are left disabled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley200157_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley200157-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a> (IARC) lists beryllium and beryllium compounds as <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_1_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 1 carcinogens">Category 1 carcinogens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has designated a <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> (PEL) in the workplace with a time-weighted average (TWA) 0.002&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>  and a constant exposure limit of 0.005&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> over 30 minutes, with a maximum peak limit of 0.025&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of constant 0.0005&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>. The <a href="/wiki/IDLH" class="mw-redirect" title="IDLH">IDLH</a> (immediately dangerous to life and health) value is 4&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The toxicity of finely divided beryllium (dust or powder, mainly encountered in industrial settings where beryllium is produced or machined) is very well-documented. Solid beryllium metal does not carry the same hazards as airborne inhaled dust, but any hazard associated with physical contact is poorly documented. Workers handling finished beryllium pieces are routinely advised to handle them with gloves, both as a precaution and because many if not most applications of beryllium cannot tolerate residue of skin contact such as fingerprints.
</p><p>Acute beryllium disease in the form of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_pneumonitis" title="Chemical pneumonitis">chemical pneumonitis</a> was first reported in Europe in 1933 and in the United States in 1943. A survey found that about 5% of workers in plants manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a> in 1949 in the United States had beryllium-related lung diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmsley20015_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmsley20015-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Chronic berylliosis resembles <a href="/wiki/Sarcoidosis" title="Sarcoidosis">sarcoidosis</a> in many respects, and the <a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">differential diagnosis</a> is often difficult. It killed some early workers in nuclear weapons design, such as <a href="/wiki/Herbert_L._Anderson" title="Herbert L. Anderson">Herbert L. Anderson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beryllium may be found in coal slag. When the slag is formulated into an abrasive agent for blasting paint and rust from hard surfaces, the beryllium can become airborne and become a source of exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-shapiro_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early researchers tasted beryllium and its various compounds for sweetness in order to verify its presence. Modern diagnostic equipment no longer necessitates this highly risky procedure and no attempt should be made to ingest this highly toxic substance.<sup id="cite_ref-deGruyter_5-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deGruyter-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Beryllium and its compounds should be handled with great care and special precautions must be taken when carrying out any activity which could result in the release of beryllium dust (<a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> is a possible result of prolonged exposure to beryllium-laden dust). Although the use of beryllium compounds in fluorescent lighting tubes was discontinued in 1949, potential for exposure to beryllium exists in the nuclear and aerospace industries and in the refining of beryllium metal and melting of beryllium-containing alloys, the manufacturing of electronic devices, and the handling of other beryllium-containing material.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A successful test for beryllium in air and on surfaces has been recently developed and published as an international voluntary consensus standard ASTM D7202. The procedure uses dilute <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_bifluoride" title="Ammonium bifluoride">ammonium bifluoride</a> for dissolution and fluorescence detection with beryllium bound to sulfonated hydroxybenzoquinoline, allowing up to 100 times more sensitive detection than the recommended limit for beryllium concentration in the workplace. Fluorescence increases with increasing beryllium concentration. The new procedure has been successfully tested on a variety of surfaces and is effective for the dissolution and ultratrace detection of refractory beryllium oxide and siliceous beryllium (ASTM D7458).<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Vauquelin, Louis-Nicolas (1798). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=dB8AAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=RA1-PA155">"De l'Aiguemarine, ou Béril; et découverie d'une terre nouvelle dans cette pierre"</a> &#91;Aquamarine or beryl; and discovery of a new earth in this stone&#93;. <i>Annales de Chimie</i>. <b>26</b>: 155–169.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annales+de+Chimie&amp;rft.atitle=De+l%27Aiguemarine%2C+ou+B%C3%A9ril%3B+et+d%C3%A9couverie+d%27une+terre+nouvelle+dans+cette+pierre&amp;rft.volume=26&amp;rft.pages=155-169&amp;rft.date=1798&amp;rft.aulast=Vauquelin&amp;rft.aufirst=Louis-Nicolas&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DdB8AAAAAMAAJ%26pg%3DRA1-PA155&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABeryllium" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a footnote on <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=dB8AAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=RA1-PA169#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">page 169</a> of (Vauquelin, 1798), the editors write:  "(1)  La propriété la plus caractéristique de cette terre, confirmée par les dernières expériences de notre collègue, étant de former des sels d'une saveur sucrée, nous proposons de l'appeler <i>glucine</i>, de γλυχυς, <i>doux</i>, γλυχύ, <i>vin doux</i>, γλυχαιτω, <i>rendre doux</i> … <i>Note des Rédacteurs</i>."  ((1)  The most characteristic property of this earth, confirmed by the recent experiments of our colleague [Vauquelin], being to form salts with a sweet taste, we propose to call it <i>glucine</i> from γλυχυς, <i>sweet</i>, γλυχύ, <i>sweet wine</i>, γλυχαιτω, <i>to make sweet</i> … <i>Note of the editors</i>.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Klaproth, Martin Heinrich, <i>Beitrage zur Chemischen Kenntniss der Mineralkörper</i> (Contribution to the chemical knowledge of mineral substances), vol. 3, (Berlin, (Germany):  Heinrich August Rottmann, 1802), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=8A8KAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA78#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">pages 78-79</a>:  "Als Vauquelin der von ihm im Beryll und Smaragd entdeckten neuen Erde, wegen ihrer Eigenschaft, süsse Mittelsalze zu bilden, den Namen <i>Glykine</i>, <i>Süsserde</i>, beilegte, erwartete er wohl nicht, dass sich bald nachher eine anderweitige Erde finden würde, welche mit völlig gleichem Rechte Anspruch an diesen Namen machen können.  Um daher keine Verwechselung derselben mit der Yttererde zu veranlassen, würde es vielleicht gerathen seyn, jenen Namen <i>Glykine</i> aufzugeben, und durch Beryllerde (<i>Beryllina</i>) zu ersetzen; welche Namensveränderung auch bereits vom Hrn. Prof. Link, und zwar aus dem Grunde empfohlen worden, weil schon ein Pflanzengeschlecht <i>Glycine</i> vorhanden ist."  (When Vauquelin conferred -- on account of its property of forming sweet salts -- the name <i>glycine</i>, <i>sweet-earth</i>, on the new earth that had been found by him in beryl and smaragd, he certainly didn't expect that soon thereafter another earth would be found which with fully equal right could claim this name.  Therefore, in order to avoid confusion of it with yttria-earth, it would perhaps be advisable to abandon this name <i>glycine</i> and replace it with beryl-earth (<i>beryllina</i>); which name change was also recommended by Prof. Link, and for the reason that a genus of plants, <i>Glycine</i>, already exists.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968538-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks1968538_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWeeks1968">Weeks 1968</a>, p.&#160;538.</span>
